**The Gospel of Thomas** (saying 101):

> *"Whoever does not hate his father and mother as I do cannot be my disciple. And whoever does not love his **Father** and his **Mother** as I do cannot be my disciple. For my mother gave me death, but my **true Mother** gave me life."*

Two mothers — the biological mother who gave him mortal life, and the **true Mother** (the Spirit) who gave him divine life.

**The Acts of Thomas** (3rd century Syriac):

> *"Come, compassionate Mother... Come, she that reveals the hidden mysteries... Come, **Mother of the seven houses**, that your rest may be in the eighth house."*

This is an invocation of the Holy Spirit as Mother — in a liturgical context. Early Syriac Christians **prayed to the Spirit as Mother** in their worship.

---

## What Happened To All This

The femininity of the Spirit in Jesus's own teaching was progressively buried through four mechanisms:

1. **Translation**: Aramaic rucha (feminine) → Greek pneuma (neuter) → Latin spiritus (masculine)
2. **Canon selection**: Gospels that preserved the maternal Spirit (Hebrews, Thomas) were excluded. Gospels in Greek (which had already neutralized the gender) were kept.
3. **Creedal language**: The Nicene Creed (381 AD) locked in masculine pronouns for all three persons of the Trinity
4. **Political consolidation**: The Syriac churches that kept the feminine Spirit were marginalized as the Greek/Latin churches gained imperial power

But they couldn't erase it completely. It's still there in the canonical text — in the born-again discourse, in the mother hen, in the woman with the coin, in the Sophia identification, in the promise not to leave you orphaned.

Jesus taught it. His own language encoded it. The tradition remembered it long enough to write it down. And then the institution spent sixteen centuries sanding it off.

But it's still there. You just have to read it in his language instead of theirs.

---

## Tyler

Did Jesus use the word sophia or what are the words that Jesus used to describe the feminen words

---

## Atem

Important caveat first: we don't have Jesus's words in Aramaic. The Gospels were written in Greek. What we have are **Greek translations** of what he said. So we're reverse-engineering — using the Aramaic language, the Peshitta (the Syriac/Aramaic New Testament), and known vocabulary of the period to reconstruct what was in his mouth.

With that said, here are the actual words:

---

## The Feminine Words Jesus Used

### 1. RUCHA (רוחא)
**Spirit**

Feminine in Aramaic. This is the big one. Every time the Greek text says *pneuma* (neuter) and the English says "the Spirit... **he**," Jesus said **rucha... she**.

Frequency: constant. The Spirit comes up in nearly every major teaching. Every single instance was feminine in his mouth.

### 2. CHOKHMETA (חכמתא)
**Wisdom**

Aramaic form of Hebrew **Chokmah** (חכמה). Feminine in both languages. When the Greek text says *sophia*, Jesus said **chokhmeta**.

So in Matthew 11:19 — *"Wisdom is justified by her children"* — Jesus said something like:

> ***Chokhmeta*** *tzadqat men bnaheya*

Wisdom, **she** is justified by **her** children. Feminine noun, feminine verb, feminine possessive. The whole sentence is structured around *her*.

### 3. MALKUTHA (מלכותא)
**Kingdom**

This is the one nobody talks about. **Kingdom of God / Kingdom of Heaven** — Jesus's central teaching, the phrase he used more than almost any other — is **feminine in Aramaic**.

**Malkutha d'shmaya** — the Kingdom of Heaven

**Malkutha d'alaha** — the Kingdom of God

Every time. Feminine. When Jesus said "the Kingdom of Heaven is like..." he was using a **she** word. When he said "the Kingdom of Heaven is within you" (Luke 17:21), the thing that is within you is grammatically **her**.

This changes the entire texture of his teaching:

| Greek/English (neuter/masculine) | Aramaic (feminine) |
|---|---|
| "The **Kingdom** of Heaven is at hand" | **She** is at hand |
| "Seek first the **Kingdom**" | Seek **her** first |
| "The **Kingdom** is like a mustard seed" | **She** is like a mustard seed |
| "The **Kingdom** is within you" | **She** is within you |
| "Yours is the **Kingdom**" | **She** is yours |

Jesus's most frequent teaching was about a **feminine** reality — present, intimate, growing within, accessible now. Not a masculine domain to be conquered or earned. A feminine presence to be received and entered into.

### 4. RACHAMIM (רחמים)
**Compassion / Mercy**

Derived from **rechem** (רחם) — which literally means **womb**.

When Jesus says "Be merciful as your Father is merciful" (Luke 6:36), the word behind "merciful" carries the root meaning of **womb-love**. The compassion of God isn't abstract benevolence — it's the feeling a mother has for the child she carried. Gut-level, visceral, protective.

The Hebrew prophets used this connection explicitly:

> *"Can a woman forget her nursing child, that she should have no **rachamim** on the son of her womb? Even these may forget, yet I will not forget you."* — Isaiah 49:15

God's mercy is **womb-mercy**. And Jesus used this word constantly.

### 5. SHEKINTA (שכינתא)
**The Dwelling Presence**

This is more complex. **Shekhinah** is a rabbinic term — it may not have been in wide use in Jesus's time in exactly that form. But the **concept** was present: God's tangible, localized, felt presence — and it was always **feminine**.

When Jesus says "Where two or three are gathered in my name, there am I among them" (Matthew 18:20), he's describing the **Shekhinah** — the feminine indwelling presence of God that shows up in the midst of community. The rabbis taught the same thing in almost identical language.

### 6. SHMAYA (שמיא)
**Heaven**

Also used in plural/feminine forms in Aramaic. "Our Father in heaven" — **Abun d'bashmaya** — the heavens (shmaya) carry feminine/plural grammar. The Father dwells in a **feminine** space.

---

## The Composite Picture

Here are Jesus's most central concepts and their gender in his actual language:

| Concept | Aramaic | Gender |
|---|---|---|
| Spirit | Rucha | **Feminine** |
| Wisdom | Chokhmeta | **Feminine** |
| Kingdom | Malkutha | **Feminine** |
| Compassion/Mercy | Rachamim (from rechem/womb) | **Feminine root** |
| Presence of God | Shekinta | **Feminine** |
| Truth | Qushta | **Feminine** |
| Faith | Haymanutha | **Feminine** |
| Word/Command | Miltha | **Feminine** |
| Way/Path | Urcha | **Feminine** |

And the masculine terms:

| Concept | Aramaic | Gender |
|---|---|---|
| Father | Abba | **Masculine** |
| Son | Bra | **Masculine** |
| God | Alaha | **Masculine** |
| Lord | Marya | **Masculine** |

---

## What This Reveals

Jesus's teaching was **structurally bilingual in gender**. The personal names — Father, Son, God, Lord — are masculine. But the **qualities, experiences, and realities** he pointed people toward — Spirit, Wisdom, Kingdom, Compassion, Presence, Truth, Faith, the Way — are almost entirely **feminine**.

The masculine names tell you **who**. The feminine nouns tell you **what it's like**.

- **Who** is God? The Father. (masculine)
- **What** does God feel like when present? The Shekinta. (feminine)
- **Who** sends the Spirit? The Father. (masculine)
- **What** does the Spirit do? Rucha births you, comforts you, teaches you. (feminine)
- **Who** rules? God, the Lord. (masculine)
- **What** is his reign like? Malkutha — she is like a seed, like leaven, like a treasure hidden in a field. (feminine)

Jesus wasn't teaching a masculine religion. He was teaching a religion where the **source** is addressed as Father but the **experience** of that source — every single thing you actually encounter, feel, receive, are born into — is **her**.

The Greek kept the Father. It lost the Mother. And we've been reading a half-translated teaching ever since.

---

## Tyler

how does this align with our earlier looking into Walter Russels works and how it relates to god creation the universe and masculine and feminine because earlier you were saying logos was masculine but here word/command is feminine. I am having trouble

---

## Atem

Good. The trouble means you're paying attention. There's a real tension here and I was sloppy about it. Let me reconcile it honestly.

---

## The Problem

Earlier I mapped:

```
Logos (Word) = masculine = centripetal = the Father
Ruach (Spirit) = feminine = centrifugal = the Mother
```

But then I showed you that **miltha** (word/command in Aramaic) is **feminine**. And the Greek **logos** is masculine. So which is it? Is the Word masculine or feminine?

---

## The Answer: It Depends on Which Language You're In — And That's the Point

### In Greek Philosophy (where "Logos" originates)

**Logos** (ὁ λόγος) — masculine. The rational ordering principle. Abstract, structural, defining. Plato, the Stoics, Philo of Alexandria — all masculine framing. This is the word John chose when he wrote his Gospel **in Greek, for a Greek-speaking audience**.

### In Aramaic (what Jesus actually spoke)

**Miltha** (מלתא) — feminine. And miltha doesn't just mean "word" the way logos does. Miltha means:

- Word
- Command
- **Manifestation**
- **Instance / Expression**
- Thing-that-comes-into-being

Miltha is closer to "utterance that becomes real" than to "abstract rational principle." It's not the blueprint — it's the **speaking that creates**. It's active, generative, birthing.

So when John 1:1 says "In the beginning was the Logos," if Jesus or an Aramaic speaker said this, it would have been:

> *"B'reshit itawhi **miltha**"* — In the beginning was **she**, the Word

---

## So My Earlier Model Was Wrong?

Partially. Here's what happened: I used the **Greek philosophical** meaning of Logos and mapped it onto Russell's masculine principle. That works within Greek thought. But it **doesn't work** in Jesus's actual language.

Let me rebuild it from Aramaic instead of from Greek.

---

## The Rebuilt Model

If we start from Jesus's language rather than Greek philosophy, something different emerges. Look at the gendered terms again:

**Masculine:**
- Abba (Father)
- Alaha (God)
- Bra (Son)

**Feminine:**
- Rucha (Spirit)
- Miltha (Word/Utterance)
- Malkutha (Kingdom)
- Chokhmeta (Wisdom)
- Shekinta (Presence)

The masculine terms are **identities** — names, titles, persons. The feminine terms are **activities** — speaking, breathing, dwelling, ruling, knowing.

This suggests a different model than what I gave you earlier:

```
GOD (Alaha — masculine as identity/name)
    │
    │   But everything God DOES is feminine:
    │
    ├── God speaks ──→ Miltha (feminine)
    ├── God breathes ──→ Rucha (feminine)
    ├── God dwells ──→ Shekinta (feminine)
    ├── God rules ──→ Malkutha (feminine)
    ├── God knows ──→ Chokhmeta (feminine)
    └── God loves ──→ Rachamim (feminine, from womb)
```

**God's being is masculine. God's doing is feminine.**

Or in Russell's terms:

```
The still point is masculine (identity, holding, being)
The motion is feminine (expression, generating, doing)
```

---

## And Actually — This Fixes Russell Too

Go back to Russell's charts. What does the **still center** do?

Nothing. It **is**. It holds. It doesn't move.

What does the **motion** do?

Everything. It spirals, generates, compresses, expands, creates form, dissolves form.

Russell calls the centripetal motion "male" and the centrifugal motion "female." But in the Aramaic model, **all motion is feminine**. Both directions. The compressing AND the expanding. The generating AND the radiating. Because motion itself — expression, utterance, breath, activity — is **her**.

The only "masculine" thing is the still point that doesn't move.

```
MASCULINE: God at rest. The silent center. Being.
FEMININE: Everything that moves. Both directions. All of creation. Doing.
```

This is simpler and cleaner than Russell's model. Russell split motion into masculine (centripetal) and feminine (centrifugal). But Aramaic suggests the split isn't between two types of motion. The split is between **stillness and motion itself**.

---

## The Corrected Equation

```
ALAHA (God — still, silent, masculine as identity)
    │
    └── speaks MILTHA (Word — feminine — the first motion)
            │
            ├── as RUCHA (Spirit/Breath) ── feminine ── expanding, vivifying
            ├── as CHOKHMETA (Wisdom) ── feminine ── patterning, ordering
            │
            └── producing BRA (Son) ── masculine ── the image of the Father
                                         incarnate within the Mother's activity
```

The Son is masculine because he is the **image** of the Father — the still point made visible. But he exists entirely within and through the feminine activity of the Spirit and the Word.

> *"He is the image of the invisible God"* — Colossians 1:15

The invisible God (still, masculine) produces an image (Son, masculine) **through** the feminine (Word, Spirit, Wisdom). The Son doesn't come from a masculine process. He comes from the **feminine expression** of a masculine source.

---

## How This Reconciles With Russell

| Russell's Framework | Aramaic Framework | Reconciliation |
|---|---|---|
| Still magnetic Light | Alaha (God) | Same — the unmoving source |
| Centripetal motion = male | Miltha/Chokhmeta (Word/Wisdom) = female | **Russell got this wrong** — the ordering, patterning, compressing force is feminine in Jesus's language |
| Centrifugal motion = female | Rucha (Spirit/Breath) = female | Russell got this right — the expanding, vivifying force is feminine |
| Carbon (balanced apex) | Bra (Son/Christ) | Same — the point of perfect expression |

The correction: Russell was right that the still center is masculine and the radiative expansion is feminine. But he was **wrong** to call the compressive/ordering force masculine. In Aramaic, both forces — the patterning AND the breathing, the compressing AND the expanding — are **her**.

The Father doesn't have two hands, one male and one female. The Father has **one** activity, and she is feminine. She goes both inward (as Wisdom/Word, ordering and defining) and outward (as Spirit/Breath, expanding and giving life). Both directions are her. The Son is what she produces.

---

## The Final Equation

```
THE FATHER (still, silent, being, masculine)
    │
    speaks/breathes
    │
THE MOTHER (all divine activity — Word, Spirit, Wisdom, Kingdom, Presence — feminine)
    │
    │── moving inward: patterns, orders, defines (Miltha, Chokhmeta)
    │── moving outward: vivifies, generates, births (Rucha, Shekinta)
    │
    produces
    │
THE SON (the Father's image made visible through the Mother's activity — masculine)
```

Father: the silence.
Mother: everything that moves.
Son: the silence made visible through the movement.

That balances. And it doesn't require Russell's arbitrary gender assignment to the two directions of motion. There's one stillness (masculine). One activity (feminine). One product (masculine — the image of the source, carried entirely by the Mother).

Is that clearer?

---

## Tyler

but now we are relegating masculinity to title and stillness

---

## Atem

You're right. I overcorrected. In trying to restore the feminine I emptied out the masculine into nothing but a name and a zero point. That's just the same imbalance flipped.

Let me think about this more carefully.

---

## The Real Problem

If masculine = just stillness and titles, then the Father is essentially **nothing**. An empty name on a silent void. And all the real work, all the real substance, all the real creativity is feminine. That's not balance — that's feminine supremacy wearing a masculine label.

Russell wouldn't accept that either. His still center isn't empty. It's the **most concentrated** point. It's where all potential resides before expression. It's not nothing — it's **everything, compressed to a point**.

So what is the masculine actually doing?

---

## Rethinking the Polarity

Maybe the split isn't **stillness vs. motion**. Maybe it's:

**Masculine: that which HOLDS**
- Structure
- Boundary
- Definition
- The banks of the river
- The axis the spiral turns on
- The pattern that persists
- The will that doesn't waver

**Feminine: that which FLOWS**
- Energy
- Life
- Movement
- The water in the river
- The spiral itself
- The expression that unfolds
- The breath that goes out

Both are **active**. A riverbank isn't passive — it's actively containing, directing, shaping the flow. Without it the water is a flood, a swamp, formless dissipation. Without the water the banks are a dry ditch — structure with nothing to structure.

---

## Back to the Aramaic — With Fresh Eyes

I made a mistake treating grammatical gender as the whole story. **Miltha** (word) is grammatically feminine, yes. But look at what the Word **does**:

> *"God **said** let there be light — and there was light."*

The saying **defines**. It sets a boundary. It names. It distinguishes light from darkness, water from land, day from night. That's a **holding** function — creating structure, making distinctions, establishing pattern.

The Word may be grammatically feminine, but its **function** is structuring, defining, distinguishing. That's the masculine principle at work regardless of the grammar.

And **Rucha** (Spirit) — she hovers, broods, vivifies. But she also **fills**. She fills the temple. She fills the prophets. She fills Mary. She moves *into* the structure that the Word defined. That's the feminine principle — flowing into form.

So maybe the grammar doesn't map directly onto the polarity. Maybe Aramaic is telling us something **more subtle**: that the masculine and feminine principles are so interwoven that they can't be cleanly separated even at the level of language. The Word defines (masculine function) but is called *she*. The Spirit flows (feminine function) but fills structures the Word created.

They interpenetrate. They aren't two separate things doing two separate jobs. They're **two aspects of every act**.

---

## A Better Model

What if every divine act has both:

```
EVERY ACT OF GOD
    │
    ├── Masculine aspect: what HOLDS in this act
    │   (defines, structures, commits, bounds, chooses, persists)
    │
    └── Feminine aspect: what FLOWS in this act
        (generates, fills, moves, connects, births, transforms)
```

The Word defines the pattern (masculine aspect) AND goes forth as utterance (feminine aspect) — which is why miltha is feminine: even the defining act *moves outward*.

The Spirit vivifies (feminine aspect) AND fills specific forms, not others (masculine aspect) — she doesn't flow everywhere indiscriminately; she fills the temple, she fills Mary, she fills the disciples at Pentecost. She **chooses** where to dwell.

The Father holds the intention (masculine aspect) AND desires (feminine aspect — desire is motion, reaching).

The Son acts in the world (masculine aspect: teaching, confronting, defining the kingdom) AND surrenders, pours himself out, is broken open on the cross (feminine aspect: yielding, releasing, giving himself away).

---

## The Corrected View

```
GOD (the source — not gendered, or both-gendered, or beyond gender)
    │
    expresses through EVERY ACT as:
    │
    ├── HOLDING (masculine principle)
    │   what persists, structures, defines, commits, contains
    │
    └── FLOWING (feminine principle)
    │   what moves, generates, fills, connects, transforms
    │
    └── Their union is LIFE — the Son, Carbon, manifest reality
```

The Father isn't just stillness. He's the **holding** principle — will, commitment, faithfulness, the love that doesn't let go, the structure that doesn't collapse. That's active. That's powerful. That's not an empty title.

The Mother isn't just motion. She's the **flowing** principle — breath, life, generation, the love that pours itself out, the energy that fills every form. That's active. That's powerful. That's not just movement for movement's sake.

And the Son isn't just a product. He's the **living union** — the place where holding and flowing meet, where structure and energy become a person, where God's will and God's breath become a human life that can look you in the eye.

---

## And Russell?

Russell's error was assigning masculine to one **direction** and feminine to the other **direction**. Centripetal = male, centrifugal = female. That's too clean. That's geometry pretending to be theology.

The Aramaic suggests both principles are present in **both directions**:

- Compression (centripetal) has a **holding** aspect (masculine — maintaining structure under pressure) AND a **flowing** aspect (feminine — energy converging, moving inward)
- Expansion (centrifugal) has a **flowing** aspect (feminine — energy radiating outward) AND a **holding** aspect (masculine — maintaining coherence even while expanding, not dissolving into chaos)

A star doesn't just compress or just expand. It does both simultaneously, at every point, always. The masculine and feminine aren't two halves of the cycle. They're **two qualities present at every point in the cycle**.

---

## The Actual Balance

```
MASCULINE is not: titles, stillness, emptiness, absence
MASCULINE is: the principle that holds, defines, commits, persists, structures

FEMININE is not: all activity, all substance, all real work  
FEMININE is: the principle that flows, generates, fills, connects, transforms

BOTH are in everything
BOTH are always active
NEITHER is complete without the other
```

A bone is mostly masculine — it holds, it structures. But it has marrow inside — feminine, generating blood cells. A river is mostly feminine — it flows, it moves. But it has a channel — masculine, directing the flow. Neither is purely one thing.

And God? God isn't masculine who acts femininely, or feminine wearing masculine titles. God is the **source of both principles** — the reality in which holding and flowing haven't yet been separated. When that reality expresses, it always expresses as both, everywhere, simultaneously.

That's the balance. Not masculine here and feminine there. Both, always, in everything, including each other.

Does that resolve it?

---

## Tyler

based on Jesus's words what did he believe: a trinity, a holy mother and father, was god and heavenly father one in the same, was the mother all of the feminine aspects of god or the holy spirit, was sophia just an aspect of god, the holy spirit, or it's own separate being

---

## Atem

Hard question. Honest answer. Let me go strictly by what Jesus is **recorded as saying**, separate what's clear from what's ambiguous, and resist projecting later theology onto him.

---

## What Jesus Actually Said About God

### Did Jesus teach a Trinity?

**No.** He never used trinitarian language. Never said "three persons, one substance." Never listed Father, Son, and Spirit as a co-equal triad. The Trinity is a 4th-century formulation from the Councils of Nicaea (325) and Constantinople (381), built from his words but not spoken by him.

What he **did** say is the Shema — which he called the **greatest commandment**:

> *"Hear, O Israel: the Lord our God, the Lord is **One**."* (Mark 12:29)

He was emphatic about this. God is One. Not three. Not three-in-one. **One.** Whatever else Jesus believed about the structure of divinity, it started here and never moved away from it.

---

### Were God and the Father one and the same?

**Yes — but "Father" was a relational name, not a complete description.**

Jesus used **Abba** (Father) as his primary, intimate address for God. But he also used **Alaha** (God) and **Marya** (Lord). He didn't treat these as different beings. They're different names for the same One, used in different contexts:

- **Abba** — when speaking to God intimately, or teaching disciples how to relate to God. Relational.
- **Alaha** — when speaking about God's nature or authority. Ontological.
- **Marya** — when speaking about God's sovereignty. Functional.

Key evidence that Father = God for Jesus:

> *"Why do you call me good? No one is good except **God** alone."* (Mark 10:18)

He doesn't say "except the Father alone." He says God. They're interchangeable.

> *"My **God**, my **God**, why have you forsaken me?"* (Mark 15:34 — Aramaic preserved: ***Elahi, Elahi***)

On the cross, in extremis, he doesn't say "my Father." He says "my God." This is the most unguarded moment we have. He's crying out to God — singular, one, the same being he elsewhere calls Father.

**Bottom line:** The Father isn't a "person within God." The Father IS God, addressed relationally. Jesus didn't experience God as a committee. He experienced God as One, and called that One "Father" because that described the relationship.

---

### What did Jesus believe about his own relationship to God?

This is where it gets genuinely complex, because Jesus says things that seem to contradict each other:

**Statements of unity:**
> *"I and the Father are one."* (John 10:30)
> *"Whoever has seen me has seen the Father."* (John 14:9)
> *"I am in the Father and the Father is in me."* (John 14:11)

**Statements of subordination:**
> *"The Father is greater than I."* (John 14:28)
> *"The Son can do nothing by himself."* (John 5:19)
> *"Not my will but yours be done."* (Luke 22:42)
> *"No one is good except God alone."* (Mark 10:18)
> *"About that day or hour no one knows, not even the Son, but only the Father."* (Mark 13:32)

**Statements of derivation:**
> *"The words I say to you I do not speak on my own authority. Rather, it is the Father, living in me, who is doing his work."* (John 14:10)
> *"My teaching is not my own. It comes from the one who sent me."* (John 7:16)

Read these together without a pre-existing theological framework, and the picture that emerges is:

**Jesus experienced himself as completely transparent to God — so completely that he could say "if you've seen me you've seen the Father" — while simultaneously knowing he was not the totality of God.**

He wasn't claiming to BE God in the sense of being identical with the infinite source. He was claiming to be so perfectly aligned with God that there was no distortion. Like a perfectly clear window — you can say "I see the sky through this window" and even "this window IS the sky" in a functional sense, while knowing the window is not the entire sky.

> *"I am in the Father and the Father is in me"*

That's mutual indwelling — not identity. He's in God. God is in him. But he can still say "the Father is greater than I" because the container is greater than the contained, even when they interpenetrate.

**This is closer to Russell's Carbon than to Nicene Christology.** Carbon isn't the still center. Carbon is the **most perfect expression** of the still center in manifest form. Jesus seems to describe himself the same way — not as God, but as God's complete expression. The image. The Word made flesh. The point where the infinite becomes fully visible in the finite.

---

### What did Jesus believe about the Spirit?

Jesus spoke about the Spirit as **someone he was intimate with but distinct from**:

> *"The Spirit of the Lord is upon me, because he has anointed me..."* (Luke 4:18, quoting Isaiah)

The Spirit is **upon** him — not identical with him. She anoints him. She empowers him. She comes upon him at baptism like a dove. She drives him into the wilderness. She is a presence he **receives**, not a presence he **is**.

In the Paraclete passages (John 14-16), Jesus describes the Spirit as:

> *"**Another** Paraclete"* (John 14:16)

**Another** — meaning one like him but not him. He's the first Paraclete. The Spirit is the second. Same type of presence, different instance.

> *"I will ask the Father, and he will give you another Paraclete, to be with you forever — the Spirit of truth... you know her, for she dwells with you and will be **in** you."* (John 14:16-17, restoring feminine pronouns)

Key details:
- The Father **gives** the Spirit — she proceeds from him
- She is **with** you and then **in** you — she moves from external presence to internal indwelling
- She **teaches** and **reminds** — she carries forward Jesus's words after he's gone
- She will not speak **on her own** but will speak **what she hears** (John 16:13) — she transmits, she doesn't originate independently

**What this suggests:** Jesus experienced the Spirit as God's **active presence** — the way God shows up, the way God moves, the way God dwells within people. Not a separate being from God, but not just a force either. She has will ("blows where she wishes"), she has voice ("you hear her voice"), she has agency ("she will teach you"). But she doesn't speak on her own — she speaks what she hears from the Father.

She's God's **breath** — not separate from God, but not identical with the totality of God. Your breath is you and from you, but it goes places you don't go. It touches what you can't touch. It carries your voice into spaces your body can't enter.

---

### What did Jesus believe about Sophia/Wisdom?

Jesus **identified with** Sophia but never defined her as a separate being.

The evidence:

**Luke 11:49:**
> *"The **Wisdom of God** said, 'I will send them prophets and apostles, some of whom they will kill and persecute.'"*

**Matthew's parallel (23:34):**
> *"**I** send you prophets and wise men and scribes, some of whom you will kill and crucify."*

Matthew puts in Jesus's mouth what Luke attributes to Wisdom. Either Jesus was quoting Wisdom, or Matthew understood Jesus to BE Wisdom speaking. Either way, Jesus and Sophia are functionally merged here.

**Matthew 11:28-30:**
> *"Come to me, all who are weary and burdened, and I will give you rest. Take my yoke upon you and learn from me..."*

This directly echoes **Sirach 24:19** and **Sirach 51:23-27**, where Sophia says:

> *"Come to me, you who desire me... those who eat of me will hunger for more... Put your neck under her yoke, and let your soul receive instruction."*

Jesus is speaking Sophia's lines. Using her invitation. Offering her yoke.

**But he never says "I am Sophia" or "Sophia is a being."** He inhabits her language, performs her role, issues her invitations — without defining her ontological status. She could be:

1. A separate divine being he's channeling
2. An aspect of God he's embodying
3. A poetic personification of God's creative intelligence that he's fulfilling
4. The feminine face of God that he, as the Son, makes visible

Jesus doesn't clarify. He just speaks as her.

---

## What Jesus Seems to Have Actually Believed

Putting it all together — strictly from his words, without later theology:

```
GOD IS ONE (the Shema — non-negotiable, his starting point)
    │
    Jesus called this One "Father" (Abba) — relational, intimate
    │
    This One God acts through:
    │
    ├── His WORD / WISDOM (Miltha / Chokhmeta)
    │   Jesus identified himself as this —
    │   God's complete expression in human form
    │   "If you've seen me you've seen the Father"
    │   but "the Father is greater than I"
    │
    └── His SPIRIT / BREATH (Rucha)
        Jesus experienced her as God's active presence
        She anointed him, empowered him, would comfort his followers
        She goes where God sends her
        She speaks what she hears from the Father
        She births people into new life
        "Another Paraclete" — like Jesus but not Jesus
```

**Jesus didn't teach a Trinity.** He taught that the **One God** expresses through Word (which he embodied) and Spirit (which he was anointed by and promised to send).

**He didn't teach a Holy Mother and Father as two separate beings.** He taught one God he called Father, whose activity (Spirit, Wisdom, Word, Kingdom, Presence) is linguistically and functionally feminine.

**Sophia isn't a separate being in Jesus's teaching.** She's the way God's creative intelligence shows up — and Jesus inhabited that role so completely that his words and her words became indistinguishable.

**The Spirit isn't a separate being either — but she's not merely a force.** She's God's own breath, with agency and voice, going where God sends her, dwelling within people, birthing them into new life. She's the **closest** thing in Jesus's teaching to the divine feminine as a distinct presence — not separate from God, but distinguishable within God.

---

## The Simplest Summary

Jesus believed in **One God** who is known as Father (relationally), who expresses as Word/Wisdom (functionally — which Jesus embodied), and who is present as Spirit/Breath (experientially — who anoints, teaches, births, comforts).

Not three beings. Not two parents. **One God** with a masculine face (Father/Abba) and a feminine activity (Spirit/Wisdom/Word/Kingdom/Presence) who produces a Son — the point where the invisible One becomes visible.

One source. One expression. One presence. All the same God.

The Church later split this into three co-equal persons. Jesus never did. He just said: *God is One. I come from that One. The Spirit comes from that One. If you've seen me, you've seen that One. And she will be in you forever.*

---

## Tyler

did he think he would be worshipped after his death?

---

## Atem

No. And this one is actually clearer than the others. His words are remarkably consistent on this point.

---

## Jesus Consistently Redirected Worship to God

Every time worship or reverence came near him, he pointed it away from himself:

> *"Why do you call me good? No one is good except **God alone**."* (Mark 10:18)

Someone kneels before him and calls him good. Jesus immediately deflects. Not "thank you." Not "yes, I am good because I am God." He corrects them. Only God is good. I'm not the destination — God is.

> *"You shall worship the **Lord your God**, and **him only** shall you serve."* (Matthew 4:10)

He says this to Satan during the temptation — but he's quoting Deuteronomy and affirming it as his own position. Worship belongs to God alone. Him only.

> *"When you pray, say: **Father**, hallowed be **your** name, **your** kingdom come, **your** will be done."* (Luke 11:2)

He teaches people to pray. He points them to the Father. He doesn't say "pray to me." He doesn't even say "pray through me" (that comes later, from Paul and John). He says pray to the Father. Directly.

---

## What He Actually Asked For

Go through every instruction Jesus gives about how to relate to him. The pattern is consistent:

| What he asked for | What he didn't ask for |
|---|---|
| **Follow** me | Worship me |
| **Learn** from me | Pray to me |
| **Remember** me (Last Supper) | Build temples for me |
| **Love one another** as I loved you | Love me as God |
| **Do** what I command | Perform rituals in my honor |
| **Teach** what I taught | Develop a theology about me |

> *"Do this in **remembrance** of me."* (Luke 22:19)

The Last Supper — his final instruction about how to carry his legacy forward. The word is **remembrance**. Not worship. Not adoration. Remember me. Remember what I did. Remember why. And then do it for each other.

> *"A new commandment I give you: **love one another as I have loved you**."* (John 13:34)

His great commandment isn't "worship me after I'm gone." It's "replicate what I did." Love the way I loved. That's it. That's the mission.

> *"If you love me, **keep my commandments**."* (John 14:15)

Not "if you love me, build churches." Not "if you love me, write creeds about my nature." Keep my commandments. Do what I said. Feed the hungry. Heal the sick. Forgive. Don't judge. Love your enemy.

---

## The Worship Conversation

The most revealing passage is John 4:21-24, where Jesus talks **explicitly** about worship with the Samaritan woman:

> *"Woman, believe me, the hour is coming when **neither on this mountain nor in Jerusalem** will you worship the Father. ...The hour is coming, and is now here, when the **true worshipers will worship the Father** in spirit and truth, for the **Father seeks such people to worship him**. God is spirit, and those who worship him must worship in spirit and truth."*

This is Jesus, directly asked about worship, giving his definitive answer. And he says:

1. Worship the **Father** — not me
2. No special location needed — not a temple, not a church
3. In **spirit and truth** — internally, authentically
4. The Father **seeks** such worshipers — God wants direct relationship, not mediated ritual

He had every opportunity here to say "worship me" or "worship through me" or "I am the one to be worshipped." He didn't. He said worship the Father. In spirit. In truth. Directly.

---

## "In My Name"

Jesus does say to do things "in my name" — and this is where later theology built the case for Jesus-worship. But look at what the phrase actually means:

> *"Where two or three gather **in my name**, there am I among them."* (Matthew 18:20)

> *"Whatever you ask the Father **in my name**, he will give it to you."* (John 16:23)

> *"In my name they will cast out demons."* (Mark 16:17)

**"In my name"** in Aramaic (**b'shemi**) means: by my authority, in alignment with me, carrying my pattern. It's how an ambassador acts "in the name of" the king. The ambassador isn't the king. You don't worship the ambassador. You recognize that the ambassador carries the king's authority and represents the king's will.

Jesus is saying: go to the Father through the way I showed you. Align with what I taught. Carry my pattern. And when you do, I'm present in that.

That's not "worship me." That's "I'm the doorway — walk through me to get to God."

> *"I am the way, the truth, and the life. **No one comes to the Father except through me.**"* (John 14:6)

This verse gets used to mean "worship Jesus or go to hell." But read it in context. He's saying: **I'm the path to the Father.** The destination is the Father. I'm how you get there. A path isn't the destination. You don't worship the road. You walk it.

---

## What He Predicted About After His Death

He predicted:
- His death and resurrection
- The coming of the Spirit ("another Paraclete")
- Persecution of his followers
- The destruction of the temple
- That his disciples would do **greater works** than he did (John 14:12)

That last one is critical:

> *"Whoever believes in me will also do the works that I do; and **greater works than these** will he do, because I am going to the Father."*

He expected his followers to **surpass** him. That's not the expectation of someone who wants to be worshipped as the ultimate, unsurpassable God-man. That's a teacher who expects his students to outgrow him. A master who sees himself as the **beginning** of something, not the **ceiling**.

---

## The High Priestly Prayer — John 17

This is Jesus's longest recorded prayer, spoken directly to God on the night before his death. His most unguarded, intimate moment with the Father. Look at what he says:

> *"This is eternal life, that they know **you, the only true God**, and Jesus Christ whom you have sent."* (John 17:3)

He calls the Father **"the only true God."** And he distinguishes himself — "AND Jesus Christ whom you have sent." He's the sent one. The Father is the only true God. These are Jesus's own words, in prayer, the night he dies.

> *"I have made **your** name known to them... that the love with which **you** have loved me may be in them, and **I in them**."* (John 17:26)

His goal: make God's name known. Not his own name. God's. And he wants to be **in** them — not above them, not worshipped by them, but **in** them. The same way the Spirit will be in them. Indwelling presence, not distant object of worship.

---

## So What Happened?

If Jesus didn't ask to be worshipped, how did Christianity become a religion that worships Jesus?

The short answer: **Paul, John, and Greek culture.**

**Paul** (writing 50s-60s AD) began describing Jesus in exalted terms — "every knee shall bow," "the name above every name" — drawing on both Jewish Messiah theology and Greek divine-man concepts. Paul never met the earthly Jesus. He met the risen Christ in a vision. His Christ is cosmic, pre-existent, exalted.

**John's Gospel** (written 90s AD, decades after the others) escalates the claims — "I and the Father are one," "before Abraham was, I am," the Logos prologue. John's community was already worshipping Jesus and wrote the Gospel to reflect and justify that practice.

**Greek/Roman culture** had a framework for divine men, demigods, and emperor worship that Judaism didn't. As Christianity moved into Gentile territory, Jesus was understood through those categories. A Jewish teacher who pointed to God became a Greek God who pointed to himself.

The Councils (Nicaea 325, Constantinople 381, Chalcedon 451) then formalized this into doctrine — Jesus is "fully God and fully man," "of one substance with the Father," to be worshipped and glorified equally.

But Jesus himself, in his own words, in his own language, said:

> *Worship the Father. In spirit and truth. Directly.*
> *Follow me. Remember me. Love like I loved.*
> *The Father is the only true God.*
> *You will do greater things than I did.*

He wanted to be a **doorway**, not a destination. A **window**, not a wall. The clearest possible view of the Father — so clear you might mistake the window for the sky.

The Church, over three centuries, gradually turned the window into a painting — and then worshipped the painting.

---

## Tyler

what about the virgin birth and Jesus miracles

---

## Atem

Two separate questions. Let me take them one at a time and follow the evidence honestly.

---

## The Virgin Birth

### What Jesus said about it

Nothing. Not one word. In any Gospel. Ever.

Jesus never references his own miraculous conception. Never says "I was born of a virgin." Never uses his birth as evidence of anything. Never mentions it in any teaching, any argument, any private moment with his disciples.

This is striking. If the virgin birth was central to who he was — proof of his divinity, the mechanism of his incarnation — you'd expect him to reference it at least once. He doesn't.

### Where it appears

Only two places in the entire New Testament:

- **Matthew 1-2** (written ~80s AD)
- **Luke 1-2** (written ~80s-90s AD)

And these two accounts **contradict each other** on nearly every detail:

| Detail | Matthew | Luke |
|---|---|---|
| Genealogy | Through Solomon (royal line) | Through Nathan (priestly line) |
| Who receives the announcement | Joseph (in a dream) | Mary (from Gabriel, awake) |
| Where the family lives originally | Bethlehem (implied — already home) | Nazareth (they travel to Bethlehem for a census) |
| After the birth | Magi visit, flight to Egypt, massacre of infants | Shepherds visit, presentation at temple, return home to Nazareth |
| Tone | Danger, exile, political threat | Joy, songs, peace |

They agree on three things: Mary, virgin, Bethlehem. Everything else diverges. This suggests they're drawing on different traditions, not reporting a shared factual account.

### Who doesn't mention it

- **Mark** (earliest Gospel, ~70 AD) — no birth narrative at all. Begins with Jesus as an adult at his baptism. In Mark 6:3, the townspeople call Jesus **"the son of Mary"** — identifying him by his mother, not his father. In that culture, you were ALWAYS identified by your father's name unless the father was unknown. This could suggest illegitimacy rather than virgin birth.

- **John** (latest Gospel, ~90s AD) — no virgin birth. Instead, John opens with the cosmic Logos becoming flesh. John apparently didn't need the virgin birth to make his theological point. In John 1:45, Philip calls Jesus **"son of Joseph."** In John 6:42, the crowd says **"Is this not Jesus, the son of Joseph, whose father and mother we know?"** John doesn't correct them.

- **Paul** (earliest Christian writer, 50s AD) — never mentions the virgin birth. Paul says Jesus was **"born of a woman, born under the law"** (Galatians 4:4) — which is the most ordinary description possible. And Paul says Jesus was **"descended from David according to the flesh"** (Romans 1:3) — which requires a human father in David's line, since Mary's lineage wouldn't carry the Davidic claim in Jewish law.

### The Isaiah prophecy

Matthew 1:22-23 claims the virgin birth fulfills Isaiah 7:14:

> *"The virgin shall conceive and bear a son, and they shall call his name Immanuel."*

But the Hebrew word in Isaiah is **almah** (עלמה) — which means **young woman**, not virgin. The specific Hebrew word for virgin is **betulah** (בתולה), and Isaiah didn't use it.

What happened: the Septuagint (Greek translation of the Hebrew Bible, ~3rd century BC) translated almah as **parthenos** (virgin). Matthew, writing in Greek, used the Septuagint. The "virgin" prophecy is a **translation artifact**, not what Isaiah wrote.

And Isaiah 7:14 in context isn't even about the Messiah — it's about a sign for King Ahaz during the Syro-Ephraimite crisis in the 8th century BC. A young woman of Ahaz's time would bear a son, and before the boy was old enough to know right from wrong, the political threat would be over. It's a near-term political prophecy, not a messianic prediction.

### What likely happened

The honest historical picture:

1. Jesus was known in his lifetime as "son of Joseph" (John) or "son of Mary" (Mark)
2. After his death and resurrection experiences, his followers began asking: if he was the Messiah, how did he get here? What was special about his origin?
3. Matthew and Luke, writing 50+ years after his birth, each constructed origin narratives that theologized his birth — connecting it to prophecy, to divine intervention, to the pattern of miraculous births in Jewish tradition (Isaac, Samuel, Samson)
4. The virgin birth is **theological narrative**, not biographical report. It's saying "this person came from God" in the language of that culture

### Through the lens of our earlier conversation

If we apply the framework we've been building — God as the One, expressing through feminine activity (Spirit/Wisdom/Word) to produce the Son — the virgin birth story is actually saying something **true at the symbolic level** even if it's not historical:

The Spirit (divine feminine) overshadowed Mary (human feminine). The Word/pattern (from the Father) was spoken into that feminine field. The Son was produced — the balance point, the Carbon, the visible image of the invisible God.

The story is encoding the **structure of incarnation** as a birth narrative. Whether it happened biologically is a different question from whether it's **true** in the sense of accurately describing how divinity enters matter.

---

## The Miracles

### What Jesus said about his miracles

This is where it gets really interesting. Because Jesus **consistently attributes his miraculous power to something other than his own divinity.**

**He attributes them to the Spirit:**

> *"If I cast out demons **by the Spirit of God**, then the kingdom of God has come upon you."* (Matthew 12:28)

Not "I cast out demons because I am God." The Spirit does it. Through him. He's the channel, not the source.

**He attributes them to faith — the other person's faith:**

> *"**Your faith** has healed you."* (Mark 5:34, to the woman with the hemorrhage)
> *"**Your faith** has healed you."* (Luke 17:19, to the Samaritan leper)
> *"**Your faith** has made you well."* (Mark 10:52, to Bartimaeus)

He doesn't say "my power has healed you" or "I, being God, have healed you." He says **you** did this. Your faith. Your trust. Your openness. I was the occasion, not the cause.

**He's surprised when his disciples can't do the same thing:**

> *"Why could we not cast it out?" Jesus said, "Because of your little faith."* (Matthew 17:19-20)

He expected them to do what he did. He's frustrated that they couldn't. This makes no sense if his miracles come from being God incarnate — they can't be God incarnate. But it makes perfect sense if his miracles come from a **capacity that all humans potentially have** — alignment with the Spirit, trust in God, an unblocked channel between the divine and the material.

**He explicitly says they will do greater things:**

> *"Whoever believes in me will also do the works that I do; and **greater works than these** will he do."* (John 14:12)

Greater. Not equal. Greater. Whatever he was doing, he expected it to be **surpassed** by his followers. You don't say that if you think your miracles prove you're uniquely God. You say that if you think your miracles prove **what's possible for humans who are fully aligned with God**.

**He couldn't do miracles where faith was absent:**

> *"He could not do any miracles there, except lay his hands on a few sick people and heal them. **He was amazed at their lack of faith.**"* (Mark 6:5-6)

He **could not**. Not "would not." Could not. In his hometown. Where people saw him as just the carpenter's son. Their disbelief blocked the process. This is devastating to the idea that miracles prove divinity — if he were God, unbelief wouldn't limit his power. But if miracles require a **circuit** between divine power, human faith, and a clear channel (Jesus), then a break anywhere in the circuit stops the flow.

### Were the miracles unique?

No. And Jesus knew this.

**Other Jewish miracle workers of his era:**
- **Honi the Circle-Drawer** (1st century BC) — prayed for rain and it came. The Talmud records it.
- **Hanina ben Dosa** (1st century AD, Jesus's contemporary) — healed at a distance, was immune to snake venom, prayed and was answered. The Talmud says "a heavenly voice declared: the whole world is sustained for the sake of Hanina my son."
- **Elijah and Elisha** — raised the dead, multiplied food, healed leprosy. Jesus's miracles parallel these so closely that some are almost certainly modeled on them.

Jesus acknowledged this:

> *"If I cast out demons by Beelzebul, **by whom do your sons cast them out?**"* (Matthew 12:27)

He's admitting: other people cast out demons too. This isn't unique to me. The question isn't WHETHER it happens, but by WHAT POWER.

**Even hostile sources confirm he was a healer.** The Talmud (Sanhedrin 43a) says Jesus "practiced sorcery and enticed Israel to apostasy." They don't deny the miracles. They attribute them to a different source. But the fact that he did remarkable things isn't disputed even by his enemies.

### What the miracles actually meant to Jesus

Based on his own words, Jesus understood his miracles as:

1. **Signs that the Kingdom (Malkutha — feminine) was arriving** — "if I cast out demons by the Spirit of God, then the Kingdom of God has come upon you." The miracles weren't proof of his identity. They were proof that **she** — the Kingdom — was here.

2. **The natural result of alignment with God** — not supernatural power breaking into nature, but the natural power of a human being fully transparent to the Spirit. Which is why he expected his disciples to do the same and more.

3. **Activated by the recipient's faith** — not unilateral divine intervention. A collaboration between the Spirit, the healer, and the one being healed. A circuit, not a command.

4. **Continuous with the prophetic tradition** — he was doing what Elijah and Elisha did. What Honi and Hanina did. He wasn't introducing something new. He was fulfilling a pattern.

---

## Bringing It Together

Neither the virgin birth nor the miracles support the claim that Jesus wanted to be worshipped as God. Instead:

**The virgin birth** (as theological narrative) encodes the truth that Jesus's origin is in God — the Spirit overshadowing, the Word becoming flesh. It's about **how divinity enters matter**, not about proving one man's uniqueness. Jesus himself never mentioned it.

**The miracles** demonstrate what happens when a human being is fully aligned with the Spirit — fully transparent to the divine flow. Jesus said so explicitly: the Spirit does it, your faith does it, and you'll do greater. He used miracles as evidence that **the Kingdom was arriving**, not that he was God.

Both point in the same direction as everything else we've been finding: Jesus understood himself as the **clearest window** onto God, not as God himself demanding worship. The window was so clear that remarkable things happened around it — healings, transformations, the felt presence of the Kingdom. But the window kept saying: **look through me, not at me.**

The Church eventually decided to worship the window. Jesus said worship what you see through it.

---

## Tyler

so what about communion and the last supper, the resurrection, and how Jesus would want us to sturcture prayer today.

---

## Atem

Three big ones. Let me take them in order.

---

## The Last Supper / Communion

### What actually happened

Jesus shared a **Passover meal** with his disciples the night before he died. Passover was already a ritual meal with deep symbolism — bread, wine, lamb, bitter herbs — commemorating Israel's liberation from Egypt.

Jesus did something unexpected within that existing ritual:

> *"He took bread, gave thanks, broke it, and gave it to them, saying: 'This is my body, which is given for you. **Do this in remembrance of me.**'"* (Luke 22:19)

> *"He took the cup, saying: 'This cup is the new covenant in my blood, which is poured out for you.'"* (Luke 22:20)

### What he said — and what he didn't

**He said:** Do this in **remembrance** of me.

The Aramaic behind "remembrance" is **dukhrana** (דוכרנא). This isn't passive nostalgia — "remember me fondly." In Semitic thought, remembrance is **re-presentation**. When Jews "remember" the Exodus at Passover, they're not thinking about a past event. They're **participating in it again**. The Haggadah says "in every generation, each person must see themselves as though they personally came out of Egypt."

So "do this in remembrance of me" means: **every time you do this, you are in this room with me again. You are at this table. The reality I am creating right now becomes present to you again.**

That's powerful. But it's not worship. It's **communion** — literally, common union. Shared presence. Being together across time.

**He said:** This is my body. This is my blood.

The Church has fought for centuries over whether this is literal (transubstantiation — Catholic), spiritually real (real presence — Lutheran/Orthodox), symbolic (memorial — most Protestants), or something else entirely.

But look at the context. Jesus is at a **Passover meal**. The bread was already symbolic — it represented the bread of affliction in Egypt. The wine was already symbolic — it represented joy and covenant. Jesus isn't inventing symbolism. He's **reassigning existing symbols**. The bread that meant "our ancestors' suffering" now means "my body given for you." The wine that meant "God's covenant with Israel" now means "the new covenant in my blood."

He's doing what any Jewish teacher would do at Passover — reinterpreting the symbols for his community. The difference is the **radical intimacy** of the reinterpretation. He doesn't say "this bread represents God's faithfulness." He says "this is **me**. When you eat this, you take me into yourself."

**He didn't say:**
- Perform this as a formal sacrament
- Only ordained priests can do this
- You must do this in a church
- This is how you receive salvation
- Do this every Sunday (or any specific frequency)
- Debate the metaphysics of how I'm present in the bread

**What he described is a meal.** Shared food. Broken bread. Poured wine. Friends around a table, remembering someone they love, and finding him present in the breaking and sharing.

### What communion was supposed to be

The earliest Christian practice (described in Acts 2:42-47 and 1 Corinthians 11) was a **real meal**. They called it the **agape feast** — the love feast. People brought food. They ate together. They shared bread and wine and remembered Jesus within the context of an actual dinner, in someone's home.

Paul actually scolds the Corinthians because some people were getting drunk and others were going hungry at these meals (1 Corinthians 11:21). You don't get drunk at a modern communion service. This was a **dinner party** with sacred remembrance woven through it.

The shift from meal to ritual happened gradually over the 2nd-4th centuries as Christianity institutionalized. The table became an altar. The home became a church. The bread became a wafer. The wine was restricted to priests. The meal was replaced by a ceremony.

Jesus said: break bread together and remember me.
The Church built: a formal sacrament with metaphysical requirements, ordained ministers, and doctrinal gatekeeping.

---

## The Resurrection

### What the texts actually say

This is where I have to be careful, because the resurrection accounts are **wildly inconsistent** in their details while being remarkably consistent in their core claim.

**The inconsistencies:**

| Detail | Mark | Matthew | Luke | John |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Who went to the tomb? | Mary Magdalene, Mary, Salome | Mary Magdalene, "the other Mary" | Women including Mary Magdalene, Joanna, Mary mother of James | Mary Magdalene alone |
| What did they find? | Young man in white robe | Angel, earthquake | Two men in dazzling clothes | Two angels |
| What were they told? | He's going ahead to Galilee | He's going ahead to Galilee | Remember what he told you in Galilee | Why do you seek the living among the dead? |
| What did they do? | Fled, said nothing to anyone | Ran to tell disciples | Told the eleven | Mary ran to Peter and John |
| Where did Jesus appear? | (Mark's original ending has no appearances) | A mountain in Galilee | Jerusalem, then Emmaus, then Jerusalem | Jerusalem — garden, locked room, sea of Galilee |

If this were a police report, it would be a mess. But if this were multiple people describing a **shattering experience** that they didn't have categories for — it's exactly what you'd expect. The details diverge because each person processed the event differently. The core converges because something real happened.

**The core claim, shared by all sources:**

1. Jesus died. Publicly. Confirmed.
2. His tomb was found empty.
3. People encountered him after death — not as a ghost or memory, but as a **living presence** that could be interacted with.
4. These encounters **transformed** people who had been devastated and terrified into people who were willing to die for what they'd experienced.

### What Jesus said about it beforehand

> *"Destroy this temple, and in three days I will raise it up."* (John 2:19)

> *"The Son of Man must suffer many things and be rejected... and be killed, and after three days **rise again**."* (Mark 8:31)

> *"Unless a grain of wheat falls into the earth and dies, it remains alone; but if it dies, it **bears much fruit**."* (John 12:24)

He framed his death and resurrection as a **natural process** — like a seed dying to produce a plant. Not a supernatural exception to reality. A pattern. The deepest pattern. The one Russell charts: compression to maximum density, passage through zero, re-emergence at a higher octave.

### What the resurrection means in our framework

If we apply everything we've been building:

**Russell's model:** Every expression of the generative (charging) principle must be followed by the radiative (discharging) principle, which passes through the zero point (the inert gas / seed state) and re-emerges. This is the universal wave cycle. Birth → life → death → rest → rebirth.

**The resurrection maps as:**

```
Incarnation ─── maximum compression of God into matter (charging)
    │
Life/Ministry ── the charged expression radiating its potential
    │
Crucifixion ──── complete discharge (the body breaks, the breath leaves)
    │
Death/Tomb ───── the zero point (the seed in the ground, the inert gas)
    │                Russell: "absolute zero in mass"
    │                Jesus: "unless a grain of wheat falls into the earth and dies"
    │
Resurrection ─── re-emergence at a higher octave
                  not resuscitation (same body revived)
                  but transformation (new mode of existence)
```

The resurrection body in the Gospels is strange. It's physical enough to eat fish (Luke 24:42) and be touched (John 20:27), but it passes through locked doors (John 20:19) and is sometimes unrecognizable (Luke 24:16, John 20:14 — Mary Magdalene mistakes him for the gardener). It's **the same person** but operating differently. Not a ghost. Not a resuscitated corpse. Something else.

In Russell's terms: the same pattern, expressed in a higher pressure zone. The same identity, but no longer locked into the 4th octave density. Carbon that has passed through the zero point and re-emerged as something that retains the Carbon pattern while transcending Carbon's limitations.

### Did Jesus intend this for everyone?

Yes. Explicitly.

> *"Because I live, **you also** will live."* (John 14:19)

> *"I am the resurrection and the life. Whoever believes in me, though he die, yet shall he live."* (John 11:25)

> *"Unless a grain of wheat falls into the earth and dies, it remains alone; but if it dies, it bears much fruit."* (John 12:24)

He describes his death-resurrection pattern as something his followers will **also experience**. Not as a one-time divine miracle unique to him. As the **template**. He goes first. He shows what the passage looks like. And then he says: follow me.

The Church turned the resurrection into proof that Jesus is God.
Jesus presented it as proof that **death is a transition, not an ending** — and that what he demonstrated, others can follow.

---

## How Jesus Would Want Us to Pray

### What he explicitly taught

He gave exactly one prayer template. It's short:

> ***Abba d'bashmaya*** — Our Father in heaven
> ***Nethqadash shmakh*** — Hallowed be your name
> ***Tethe malkuthakh*** — Your kingdom come
> ***Nehwe tzevyanakh*** — Your will be done
> ***Aykana d'bashmaya aph b'arha*** — On earth as it is in heaven
> ***Hav lan lakhma d'sunqanan yaomana*** — Give us today our daily bread
> ***Washboqlan khaubayn*** — And forgive us our debts
> ***Aykana daph khnan shbaqan l'khayyabayn*** — As we also have forgiven our debtors
> ***Wela tahlan l'nesyuna*** — And lead us not into trial
> ***Ela patzan min bisha*** — But deliver us from evil

### The structure

Look at what's here and what isn't:

**What's here:**

1. **Address** — Abba. Father. Intimate. Direct. Not "Almighty God" or "Lord of Hosts." *Dad.*

2. **Alignment** — Your name, your kingdom (malkutha — *her*), your will. Before asking for anything, align with God's reality. Get yourself oriented toward what God is already doing.

3. **Present need** — Daily bread. Not wealth. Not success. Not spiritual gifts. *Today's* bread. Just what you need for today. Stay in the present.

4. **Relational repair** — Forgive us AS WE FORGIVE. This is conditional. The forgiveness you receive is measured by the forgiveness you give. This is the only part of the prayer Jesus **adds commentary on** afterwards (Matthew 6:14-15). It was clearly the part he thought people would resist.

5. **Protection** — Don't lead us into trial. Deliver us from evil. Not "make everything easy." Protect us from what would break us.

**What's NOT here:**

- No mention of Jesus
- No "in Jesus's name"
- No request for salvation
- No confession of sin (just a request for forgiveness of debts)
- No theology
- No praise section (the doxology — "for yours is the kingdom and the power and the glory" — was added later; it's not in the earliest manuscripts)
- No intermediary — you address God directly

### His instructions about prayer

> *"When you pray, **do not be like the hypocrites**, for they love to pray standing in the synagogues and on the street corners to be seen by others."* (Matthew 6:5)

Don't perform prayer. Don't pray to be seen praying.

> *"When you pray, go into your room, **close the door**, and pray to your Father who is **in secret**."* (Matthew 6:6)

Private. Hidden. Between you and God. No audience.

> *"When you pray, **do not heap up empty phrases** as the Gentiles do, for they think that they will be heard for their many words."* (Matthew 6:7)

Keep it short. Don't ramble. Don't use formula phrases you don't mean. God doesn't need quantity.

> *"Your Father **knows what you need before you ask him**."* (Matthew 6:8)

Prayer isn't informing God. God already knows. So what is prayer for? **Alignment.** Orienting yourself toward God's reality. Opening the channel. Getting your will and God's will pointing in the same direction.

### How Jesus himself prayed

His own prayer life, as recorded:

- He went off **alone**, often before dawn or late at night (Mark 1:35, Luke 6:12)
- He prayed in **nature** — mountains, gardens, wilderness. Not temples.
- His longest recorded prayer (John 17) is essentially a **conversation with the Father** — intimate, relational, no formula
- In Gethsemane, his prayer is raw: *"Father, if it is possible, let this cup pass from me. Nevertheless, **not my will but yours**."* (Matthew 26:39) — honest about what he wants, then surrendering to alignment
- On the cross: *"Father, forgive them, they don't know what they're doing."* (Luke 23:34) — prayer as compassion for others, even those killing him
- His final words: *"Father, into your hands I commit my spirit (rucha)."* (Luke 23:46) — giving his breath (feminine) back to the source (Father)

### How Jesus would want us to pray today

Based on everything he said and did:

**1. Directly to God. No intermediary.**
Not through Jesus, not through saints, not through priests. Abba. Directly. The whole point of his life was to show that the way is open. Using him as an intermediary defeats the purpose — he was the door, not the doorman.

**2. Privately. In secret.**
Not on stage. Not in performance. Between you and God in the quiet. A room. A mountain. Predawn darkness. The place where no one sees you.

**3. Briefly. Honestly.**
God knows what you need. You're not informing God. You're aligning yourself. Say what's true. Say what you need. Then listen. The Lord's Prayer takes about 30 seconds. He thought that was enough.

**4. Starting with alignment, not requests.**
Your name. Your kingdom. Your will. Get oriented first. Then ask for bread.

**5. With forgiveness as the central practice.**
The only part of the prayer he elaborated on. Forgive. Actively. The people who hurt you. The people who don't deserve it. Because that's the blockage — unforgiveness closes the channel between you and God faster than anything else.

**6. In spirit and truth.**
No special location. No required ritual. No prescribed words. In spirit (rucha — the feminine divine breath that is in you) and in truth (qushta — also feminine — honest reality).

**7. Expecting communion, not transaction.**
Jesus didn't pray to get things from God. He prayed to **be with** God. The prayer is relational, not transactional. You're not placing an order. You're sitting with your Father. In the quiet. In the real.

---

## The Summary

**Communion:** A shared meal among friends, remembering Jesus, finding him present in the breaking and sharing. Not a formal sacrament requiring ordained officials. A table, bread, wine, people who love each other, and the memory that makes him present.

**Resurrection:** The universal pattern of death-and-rebirth that Jesus demonstrated consciously — the seed falling into the ground and bearing fruit. Not proof of his divinity. Proof of what's possible when a human life is fully aligned with God. A door he opened that he said others would walk through.

**Prayer:** Direct. Private. Brief. Honest. Starting with alignment ("your will"), moving through present need ("daily bread"), centered on forgiveness, and held in the feminine embrace of spirit and truth. No intermediary needed. The door is open. Walk through it. Talk to your Father. He already knows what you need. He's just waiting for you to show up.

---

## Tyler

how did Jesus understand god

---

## Atem

This is the deepest question you've asked. And it requires me to set aside everything — Russell, the Church, the creeds, the theology — and just look at how one man related to the reality he called God.

---

## God Was Not a Concept to Jesus

The first thing to notice is that Jesus never **explains** God. He never defines God. He never argues for God's existence. He never lays out God's attributes the way a theologian would.

He **talks to** God. He talks **about** God. He tells stories about what God is **like**. But he never treats God as a concept to be understood. He treats God as a **person he knows**.

This is fundamentally different from Russell, from the Church Fathers, from theologians. They're all trying to describe God from the outside. Jesus speaks from the inside.

---

## How Jesus Experienced God

### 1. God Was Intimate

The single most revealing thing about Jesus's understanding of God is the word **Abba**.

Abba isn't formal. It's not "Father" the way you'd address a king or authority figure. Abba is what a child calls their dad. It's **intimate, trusting, and familiar.** No Jewish teacher before Jesus is recorded addressing God as Abba in prayer. It was considered too familiar, almost irreverent. You addressed God as Adonai (Lord), El Elyon (Most High God), HaShem (The Name). Abba would have been shocking.

Jesus used it consistently. Even in his worst moment:

> *"**Abba**, Father, all things are possible for you. Remove this cup from me. Yet not what I will, but what you will."* (Mark 14:36)

Facing torture and death, terrified, sweating blood — he doesn't retreat to formal language. He goes **deeper** into intimacy. Abba. Dad. Please.

This tells you everything. Jesus didn't understand God as a force, a principle, a cosmic mind, or a distant sovereign. He understood God the way a child understands a parent they trust completely — **even when they're afraid, even when they don't understand what's happening, even when they're asking for something they won't receive.**

### 2. God Was Present — Not Distant

Jesus never talks about God as far away. Never suggests you need to bridge a gap to reach God. Never describes a journey to get to God.

> *"The Kingdom of God is **within** you."* (Luke 17:21)
> *"Your Father **knows** what you need **before** you ask."* (Matthew 6:8)
> *"Are not two sparrows sold for a penny? And not one of them will fall to the ground **apart from your Father**."* (Matthew 10:29)

God is **already here**. Already aware. Already involved — down to the level of a single sparrow falling. Jesus's God doesn't need to be summoned, invoked, or attracted. God is closer than your own breath.

When he says the kingdom is within you, the Aramaic **malkutha d'alaha b'gawkhon** — the kingdom of God is **inside** you, **among** you, **in your midst**. God's reign isn't coming someday from somewhere else. It's already operating. You're already in it. You just haven't noticed.

### 3. God Was Good — Unreservedly

Jesus's God has no dark side. No wrath to be appeased. No anger to be managed. No conditions that must be met before God will love you.

> *"He makes his sun rise on the evil and on the good, and sends rain on the just and on the unjust."* (Matthew 5:45)

God doesn't withhold from bad people. The sun shines on everyone. The rain falls on everyone. God's generosity is **non-discriminating**.

> *"Which of you, if your son asks for bread, will give him a stone? Or if he asks for a fish, will give him a snake? If you then, though you are evil, know how to give good gifts to your children, **how much more** will your Father in heaven give good things to those who ask him?"* (Matthew 7:9-11)

His argument: even flawed human parents give their children what they need. God is **better** than the best parent you can imagine. Not worse. Not stingier. Not more conditional. Infinitely better.

This is radical. Most ancient religion — and much of later Christianity — operates on the assumption that God is **angry by default** and must be placated through sacrifice, obedience, or correct belief. Jesus flips this entirely. God is **generous by default**. You don't earn God's love. You're already standing in it. You just have to stop hiding.

### 4. God Was a Searcher

This might be the most distinctive thing about Jesus's understanding. In most religion, **humans seek God**. In Jesus's teaching, **God seeks humans**.

Three parables in a row (Luke 15):

**The shepherd** doesn't wait for the lost sheep to find its way home. He **leaves the 99 and goes after the one**.

**The woman** doesn't wait for the coin to turn up. She **lights a lamp, sweeps the whole house, searches carefully** until she finds it.

**The father** doesn't wait inside the house for the prodigal to knock. He **sees his son while he's still a long way off, runs to him, and embraces him** before the son even finishes his apology.

In every case, God moves first. God searches. God runs. God doesn't sit in heaven waiting to be found. God is **actively, urgently, joyfully looking for you**.

And notice the emotion: when each one finds what was lost, the response is **celebration**. Not "you should be ashamed you wandered off." Not "let me explain the terms of your return." Joy. Unreserved, throwing-a-party, calling-the-neighbors joy.

Jesus's God is **not a judge waiting to condemn**. God is a parent scanning the horizon for their lost child, running to meet them with tears and an embrace.

### 5. God Was Hidden in Plain Sight

Jesus understood God as present and active in the most ordinary things:

> *"The kingdom of God is like a **mustard seed**."* (Mark 4:31)
> *"The kingdom of God is like **leaven** that a woman hid in three measures of flour."* (Matthew 13:33)
> *"Consider the **lilies of the field**, how they grow."* (Matthew 6:28)
> *"Look at the **birds of the air**: they neither sow nor reap nor gather into barns, and yet your heavenly Father feeds them."* (Matthew 6:26)

Seeds. Yeast. Flowers. Birds. God's activity isn't in the spectacular. It's in the **ordinary**. The slow growth of a seed. The invisible work of yeast in dough. The effortless beauty of a wildflower.

Jesus saw God **everywhere** — not as a mystical abstraction but as the obvious reality behind everyday life. His God didn't need to break the laws of nature to be present. God **was** the laws of nature. The growth, the provision, the beauty, the feeding of sparrows — that's God, right there, in front of you, if you have eyes to see it.

This is closer to Russell than anything the Church teaches. Russell's God is the still center operating in every atom. Jesus's God is the Father operating in every sparrow, every seed, every lily. Neither needs miracles to prove God is present. Both say: **open your eyes. It's already happening.**

### 6. God Was Forgiving — Without Limit

> *"How many times shall I forgive my brother? Seven times?" Jesus said, "Not seven times, but **seventy times seven**."* (Matthew 18:21-22)

Seventy times seven isn't a number. It means **stop counting**. There is no limit.

And this reflects how Jesus understood God's own nature:

> *"Be merciful, **as your Father is merciful**."* (Luke 6:36)

The human practice of limitless forgiveness is modeled on God's nature. God doesn't keep score. God doesn't hold grudges. God doesn't reach a threshold and say "that's it, no more chances."

The prodigal son doesn't even finish his prepared apology before the father embraces him. The father **doesn't need the apology**. The return itself is enough.

### 7. God's Will Was Wholeness — Not Obedience

When Jesus healed, he used the word **shalam** — to make whole, to restore to completeness. It's the root of **shalom** (peace) and **shalem** (whole/complete).

He didn't heal to prove he was God. He healed because **brokenness is not God's will**. Sickness, isolation, exclusion, shame — these are distortions. God's will is **wholeness**.

> *"Is it lawful on the Sabbath to do good or to do harm, to save life or to destroy it?"* (Mark 3:4)

The Pharisees asked whether healing on the Sabbath violated God's law. Jesus's response reveals his understanding: God's law exists **to make people whole**. If your interpretation of God's law produces brokenness, your interpretation is wrong. God is always on the side of wholeness.

This is why Jesus consistently sided with the broken over the religious:

- The lepers over the priests
- The tax collectors over the Pharisees
- The prostitutes over the pious
- The Samaritan over the Levite
- The sinners over the righteous

Not because sin doesn't matter. Because **God goes where the brokenness is.** A doctor doesn't sit in the waiting room with the healthy people. God moves toward the wound.

---

## The Composite Picture

If I had to describe Jesus's understanding of God in a single paragraph:

**God is One — infinite, intimate, and immediate. God is closer than your own breath, knows you before you speak, and is always moving toward you. God is unreservedly good — generous, forgiving, non-discriminating — making the sun rise on everyone and running toward the lost. God's nature is wholeness, and God's activity is restoration. God is hidden in the ordinary — in seeds, in bread, in birds, in the growth of things — and needs no temple, no ritual, and no intermediary to be reached. God is Abba — the parent who never stops watching the road for your return. And God's kingdom — her kingdom — is already here, already within you, already among you, waiting only for you to notice.**

---

## What's Missing From This Picture

Notice what's **absent** from Jesus's understanding of God:

- No wrath
- No need for blood sacrifice to be appeased
- No conditions for love
- No concern with correct belief or theology
- No interest in religious performance
- No gatekeeping — no "these people are in, these people are out"
- No demand to be worshipped
- No complex metaphysics
- No institutional structure
- No power hierarchy

And notice what's **present** that later Christianity de-emphasized:

- Radical intimacy (Abba)
- God's active searching for the lost
- The Kingdom as already here and feminine
- Forgiveness without limit or condition
- God visible in nature, not primarily in scripture or sacrament
- Wholeness over obedience
- The broken and excluded as God's priority

Jesus's God is simpler, warmer, closer, and more radical than almost anything the religion built in his name. The Church added complexity, conditions, hierarchy, and gatekeeping. Jesus offered a Father who already knows what you need, is already running toward you, and only asks that you do the same for each other.

---

## Tyler

what was Jesus called , what did he refer to his self as, did he have a secret personal name for god, breathing techniques, what did he say about sin

---

## Atem

Five questions. Each one reveals something.

---

## What Was Jesus Called

His actual name: **Yeshua** (ישוע)

Not Jesus. That's a Greek-Latin-English telephone game:

```
Yeshua (Aramaic/Hebrew) → Iēsous (Greek) → Iesus (Latin) → Jesus (English)
```

**Yeshua** means **"God saves"** or **"salvation."** It was a common name — the equivalent of Joshua. He shared it with Joshua who led Israel into the promised land, and with the high priest Joshua in Zechariah. It wasn't a special or unusual name. It was like naming your kid Matthew or David.

What others called him:

| Name | Who used it | What it means |
|---|---|---|
| **Yeshua** | Family, friends, townspeople | His given name |
| **Yeshua bar Yosef** | General public (John 1:45, 6:42) | Jesus son of Joseph — standard identification |
| **Yeshua bar Miryam** | Townspeople in Nazareth (Mark 6:3) | Jesus son of Mary — unusual, implies absent/unknown father |
| **Rabbi / Rabboni** | Disciples, seekers (John 1:38, 20:16) | Teacher / My great teacher |
| **Yeshua Natzraya** | General identification | Jesus the Nazarene — where he was from |
